        <!-- Content well -->
        <div class="gridColumn six">
         <div class="contentWell">
          <div id="blog-header">
           <a href="http://blogs.discovermagazine.com/badastronomy/" style="display:block;width:100%;height:100%;">
            <div class="avatar">
             <img alt="" src="	

http://blogs.discovermagazine.com/badastronomy/wp-content/themes/discoverblog/images/blog_icons/large/plait.png"/>
            </div>
            <div class="words">
             <h1>
              Bad Astronomy
             </h1>
             <p class="credit">
             </p>
            </div>
           </a>
          </div>
          <div class="singlepost">
           <div class="post" id="post-main-20675">
            <div class="entry">
             <div class="navigation">
              <span class="prev">
               
               <a href="http://blogs.discovermagazine.com/badastronomy/2010/09/09/stargazing-vacations/" rel="prev">
                Stargazing vacations
               </a>
              </span>
              <span class="next">
               <a href="http://blogs.discovermagazine.com/badastronomy/2010/09/09/bad-universe-to-premiere-in-canada-eh/" rel="next">
                Bad Universe to premiere in Canada! (eh?)
               </a>
               
              </span>
             </div>
             <h1 class="post-title single">
              <a href="http://blogs.discovermagazine.com/badastronomy/2010/09/09/side-view-of-the-moon/" rel="bookmark" title="Permanent Link to Side view of the Moon!">
               Side view of the Moon!
              </a>
             </h1>
             <div class="meta">
              <span class="meta-date">
               By
               <a href="http://discovermagazine.com/authors?name=Phil+Plait" style="color:#555">
                Phil Plait
               </a>
               | 
		September 9, 2010 10:00 am
              </span>
             </div>
             <div class="shareIcons" style="border-top:1px solid #999;border-bottom:1px solid #999;padding:7px 0 7px 0;margin-bottom:20px;">
              <div class="addthis_toolbox addthis_default_style addthis_32x32_style">
               <a class="addthis_button_preferred_1">
               </a>
               <a class="addthis_button_preferred_2">
               </a>
               <a class="addthis_button_preferred_3">
               </a>
               <a class="addthis_button_preferred_4">
               </a>
               <a class="addthis_button_compact">
               </a>
               <a class="addthis_counter addthis_bubble_style">
               </a>
              </div>
             </div>
             <p>
              Sometimes being stuck on Earth is a bummer. We miss a lot of stuff! Like, frinstance, this view of the Moon
              <em>
               which is literally impossible to get from the surface of the Earth
              </em>
              :
             </p>
             <p>
              <a href="http://lroc.sese.asu.edu/news/uploads/wacorth90east4km_thumb.png" target="_blank">
               <img alt="lroc_moon_sideview" class="aligncenter size-full wp-image-20674" height="610" src="http://blogs.discovermagazine.com/badastronomy/files/2010/09/lroc_moon_sideview.jpg" width="610"/>
              </a>
             </p>
             <p>
              It looks like the Moon, doesnt it? But it also looks
              <em>
               different
              </em>
              . Thats because this mosaic of 3700+ images shows the Moon as if you were seeing it from above its east side  like you were hovering above it and following it as it orbits the Earth!
             </p>
             <p>
              <a href="http://lroc.sese.asu.edu/news/index.php?/archives/278-Moon-Seen-From-the-East.html" target="_blank">
               The images
              </a>
              were taken with the Lunar Reconnaissance Orbiters Wide Angle Camera, which can snap between roughly 50-100 km (30  60 miles) of lunar landscape in one image. As LRO circles the Moon, the camera builds up a map of the entire surface, but only one narrow strip at a time. Astronomers can then use those images to create a mosaic of the Moon as seen from any angle but its not easy.
              <br/>
              <span id="more-20675">
              </span>
              <br/>
              To get the mosaic to look right, you cant just take an image from one spot of the Moon and stitch it onto an image from another. The lighting angle will be different, for one thing, making shadows go all wonky. Also, the LRO camera points straight down, so whatever is directly beneath it will be close by, while something off to the side will be farther away. Stuff farther away will look smaller, and you have to correct for that as well.
             </p>
             <p>
              Its taken a while, but LRO scientists have figured out how to correct for all that, and are now able to make these cool maps. The big remaining issue are those missing strips, spots the camera missed because another camera was being used at the time. But still, its pretty amazing they can make this map at all.
             </p>
             <p>
              And why is it different from what we see on Earth? The Moon spins almost exactly once for every time it orbits the Earth 
              <a href="http://www.badastronomy.com/bad/misc/tides.html" target="_blank">
               thats a natural consequence of the effect of the Earths gravity on the Moon over time
              </a>
              . That means we only see one half of the Moon, and the other half is always pointing away from us.
             </p>
             <p>
              This LRO image shows the east side of the Moon which is the hemisphere of the Moon facing
              <em>
               away
              </em>
              from its direction of orbital motion (it may help to read
              <a href="http://blogs.discovermagazine.com/badastronomy/2010/09/04/just-in-case-you-need-reminding-how-nice-a-place-to-live-earth-is/" target="_blank">
               a description I wrote of this for a moon of Saturn
              </a>
              ). As seen from this perspective, the Earth is off the image to the left. So everything on the left half of this picture we can see from Earth, and everything on the right we cant
              <sup>
               <a href="#footnote">
                *
               </a>
              </sup>
              .
             </p>
             <p>
              Over the course of time the black strips will get filled in, and well have something very cool indeed: a complete map of the lunar surface at high resolution, roughly 150 meters/pixel (which is better than we can do here from Earth and is somewhat better than we can get
              <a href="http://blogs.discovermagazine.com/badastronomy/2008/08/12/moon-hoax-why-not-use-telescopes-to-look-at-the-landers/" target="_blank">
               observing the Moon with Hubble
              </a>
              ). This will be an invaluable tool to future lunar explorers and itll also make for some pretty awesome pictures. And itll also open up even more possibilities
              <a href="http://www.moonzoo.org/" target="_blank">
               for people to help astronomers get more accurate ages for the lunar surface
              </a>
               and in the meantime you can amuse yourself
              <a href="http://wms.lroc.asu.edu/lroc_browse/view/orth90east400m" target="_blank">
               zooming in on an interactive LRO map
              </a>
              with a resolution up to 400 meters/pixel.
             </p>
             <p>
              <font size="-2">
               <em>
                NASA/GSFC/Arizona State University
               </em>
              </font>
             </p>
             <p>
              <a name="footnote">
              </a>
              <br/>
             </p>
             <hr align="left" width="30%"/>
             <font size="-2">
              <em>
               <sup>
                *
               </sup>
               Actually, (as usual) the situation is a little more complicated than that; the Moon appears to rock gently left and right over the course of its orbit. This is called libration and it allows us to see a few extra percent of the lunar surface on the farside. Its a small effect though.
              </em>
             </font>
             <br/>
             <div style="clear:both;">
             </div>
             <div class="categories">
              <span class="more-about">
               CATEGORIZED UNDER:
              </span>

              </a>
             </div>

              </a>

              <a href="http://discovermagazine.com/tags/?tag=moon">

              </a>

              <a href="http://discovermagazine.com/tags/?tag=wac">

              </a>

                    Off-topic: It would be nice to have a way for commenters to send a comment or suggestion
                    <br/>
                    which is not part of a particular thread.  Since that apparently doesnt exist, Im (ab)using
                    <br/>
                    a normal comment to do so.
                   </p>
                   <p>
                    Skepticism here is correctly directed against anti-vaxxers and others whose wrong ideas
                    <br/>
                    cause actual harm to people.  However, from an intellectual point of view, all forms of
                    <br/>
                    baloney, voodoo, flim-flam etc should be given the skeptical ridicule.
                   </p>
                   <p>

                    <br/>
                    and off-the-wall stuff in regard to tuning their hi-fi systems.  Some of this, of course, makes
                    <br/>
                    sense, like acoustically isolating loudspeakers and vinyl-recordplayers and cutting down
                    <br/>
                    the reflectivity of the walls.  Other stuff, like special cables, is easily shown to be
                    <br/>
                    ineffective, not to mention various devices to de-magnetize CDs etc.

                   <p>
                    Gus Snarp  theyve known in principle how to do this for a long time, but the devils in the details.  Its not so much knowing how to do it as working out the exact math to deform each particular strip in exactly the right way to correct that particular strip for where it will be placed in the final picture.  Its tedious work.  Im sure they used some degree of automation, but theres still gonna be a lot of manual work to definite exactly how each picture needs to be adjusted until you can build the mosaic.
                   </p>

                   </p>
                   <p>
                    Also its the
                    <em>
                     far
                    </em>
                    side of the moon, not the dark side.  All sides of the moon get just as much light as the near side. (Actually, technically the far side gets MORE light in total, because it never gets shadowed by the Earth during an eclipse when it would otherwise be lit.)

                   <p>
                    Part of the reason there are fewer visible craters on the side we can see is that the magma flows (IIRC) of the maria have filled in craters. Of course that begs the question of why all the volcanic activity is (was) on the side towards Earthoh, probably tidal stresses. And Earth would provide something of a shield, for that matter  far from a perfect one, but a certain number of impactors that would otherwise hit the Moon would hit Earth instead (or burn up in atmosphere).
                   </p>

                    <i>
                     Its for pretty much the same reason that the top of your umbrella gets wetter than the bottom.
                    </i>
                   </p>
                   <p>
                    Also its the far side of the moon, not the dark side.
                   </p>
                   <p>
                    Actually there is nothing wrong with the Dark Side.  It is a
                    <i>
                     name
                    </i>
                    that has been used for a very long time.   But even literally it is not false because the term dark did not refer to lack of light, but rather to another meaning of the word.  Dark in this context means unknown.     When Dark Africa was used in the 19th Century they were not referring to the day/night cycle or to the skin color of the natives, they were simply saying it was unexplored and/or unknown.
                   </p>
                   <p>
                    Maybe I should start accusing those who use the words sunrise and sunset of falsely claiming the Sun orbits the Earth.
                   </p>

               <li class="comment odd alt thread-odd thread-alt depth-1" id="li-comment-253099">
                <div class="commentwrap clearfix">
                 <div class="commentBox clearfix" id="comment-253099">
                  <div class="comment-author vcard">
                   <img alt="" class="avatar avatar-84 photo" height="84" src="http://0.gravatar.com/avatar/676663a574e2f1038ccb81b7d2da2ac0?s=84&amp;d=http%3A%2F%2F0.gravatar.com%2Favatar%2Fad516503a11cd5ca435acc9bb6523536%3Fs%3D84&amp;r=G" width="84"/>
                   <cite class="fn">
                    Wayne on the plains
                   </cite>
                  </div>
                  <div class="commenttext">
                   <p>
                    While one side of the moon faces us and that side has more maria, one did not cause the other to happen. I think it would be more accurate to say that both characteristics have a common cause  that the interior of the moon is asymmetric. This asymmetry both caused the heavy side to face the Earth and caused more volcanic activity which erased many smaller craters. Also, the far side isnt more cratered due to more impacts, but because the surface is older (so it remembers more impacts).
                   </p>
                   <div class="comment-meta commentmetadata">
                    <span class="commentdate">

               <li class="comment even thread-even depth-1" id="li-comment-253100">
                <div class="commentwrap clearfix">
                 <div class="commentBox clearfix" id="comment-253100">
                  <div class="comment-author vcard">
                   <img alt="" class="avatar avatar-84 photo" height="84" src="http://1.gravatar.com/avatar/b448968447b420889adf2203c944d2f9?s=84&amp;d=http%3A%2F%2F1.gravatar.com%2Favatar%2Fad516503a11cd5ca435acc9bb6523536%3Fs%3D84&amp;r=G" width="84"/>
                   <cite class="fn">
                    Chris A.
                   </cite>
                  </div>
                  <div class="commenttext">
                   <p>
                    One of my pet peeves is when an image of the nearly-full Moon taken from Apollo 11 (
                    <a href="http://nssdc.gsfc.nasa.gov/imgcat/hires/a11_h_44_6667.gif" rel="nofollow">
                     http://nssdc.gsfc.nasa.gov/imgcat/hires/a11_h_44_6667.gif
                    </a>
                    ) is used as a stand-in for the Moon as seen from Earth.  Most commonly, I see it on news broadcasts during the weather segment, where they are using a Moon image to indicate a nighttime forecast.  It drives me nuts to see the Moon, with Mare Crisium almost dead-center, half covered by clouds.  Its a view NEVER seen from Earth.
                   </p>
                   <p>
                    Why should I care?  To me, its roughly the equivalent of showing an image of Saturn as seen from above one of its poles, with the rings making a bullseye, to represent the view of Saturn through a telescope from Earth.  Its simply wrong, and builds an incorrect mental picture of the universe in anyone who doesnt know better (which is most people).
                   </p>
                   <div class="comment-meta commentmetadata">
                    <span class="commentdate">
                     <a href="http://blogs.discovermagazine.com/badastronomy/2010/09/09/side-view-of-the-moon/#comment-253100">

               <li class="comment even thread-even depth-1" id="li-comment-253108">
                <div class="commentwrap clearfix">
                 <div class="commentBox clearfix" id="comment-253108">
                  <div class="comment-author vcard">
                   <img alt="" class="avatar avatar-84 photo" height="84" src="http://1.gravatar.com/avatar/b40b94101a8e6210905af41b4e60b8b4?s=84&amp;d=http%3A%2F%2F1.gravatar.com%2Favatar%2Fad516503a11cd5ca435acc9bb6523536%3Fs%3D84&amp;r=G" width="84"/>
                   <cite class="fn">
                    robbak
                   </cite>
                  </div>
                  <div class="commenttext">
                   <p>
                    @Aputsiaq: No. The same side of the moon will always face earth, baring a large impact that makes it spin.
                    <br/>
                    The moons spin is tidally locked  meaning that the moon, for many eons, was spinning with respect to the earth, but that spin was slowed down by tides in the moons rocks: Earths gravity was enough to squash, squeeze and even liquefy the moons rocks. When the moon stopped spinning, it would have rocked back and forth until its heavier side pointed to earth.
                    <br/>
                    There should be a remnant of that rocking motion remaining. I dont know how much it is, or if it is measurable.
                   </p>
                   <div class="comment-meta commentmetadata">
                    <span class="commentdate">
                     <a href="http://blogs.discovermagazine.com/badastronomy/2010/09/09/side-view-of-the-moon/#comment-253108">
                      September 9, 2010 at 11:29 pm
                     </a>
                    </span>
                    <span class="reply">
                    </span>
                   </div>
                  </div>

               <!-- #comment-## -->
               <li class="comment odd alt thread-odd thread-alt depth-1" id="li-comment-253109">
                <div class="commentwrap clearfix">
                 <div class="commentBox clearfix" id="comment-253109">
                  <div class="comment-author vcard">
                   <img alt="" class="avatar avatar-84 photo" height="84" src="http://0.gravatar.com/avatar/a5f3df5d5fb117a9769ede4dd68bf9d1?s=84&amp;d=http%3A%2F%2F0.gravatar.com%2Favatar%2Fad516503a11cd5ca435acc9bb6523536%3Fs%3D84&amp;r=G" width="84"/>
                   <cite class="fn">
                    EricH
                   </cite>
                  </div>
                  <div class="commenttext">
                   <p>
                    @robbak: The Earth does appear to move east to west and back in the Moons sky, over the course of the lunar cycle. This happens because of the eccentricity of the Moons orbit, which means that it moves around the Earth slightly faster than it rotates, for part of the month, and slightly slower the rest of the month. (There is also north-south motion, because of the Moons axial tilt.) IIRC, neither component amounts to more than 12 degrees of angle, so the Earth will always be found in the Moons sky within a box 12 degrees on a side. (And its probably less than that, but Im having trouble quickly locating any references with hard numbers, so Im relying on my memory.)
                   </p>
                   <div class="comment-meta commentmetadata">
                    <span class="commentdate">
                     <a href="http://blogs.discovermagazine.com/badastronomy/2010/09/09/side-view-of-the-moon/#comment-253109">
                      September 10, 2010 at 1:11 am
                     </a>
                    </span>
                    <span class="reply">

                    </p>

               <li class="comment odd alt thread-odd thread-alt depth-1" id="li-comment-253125">

                  <div class="comment-author vcard">
                   <img alt="" class="avatar avatar-84 photo" height="84" src="http://0.gravatar.com/avatar/062a101d155b0a746a04df848a07943f?s=84&amp;d=http%3A%2F%2F0.gravatar.com%2Favatar%2Fad516503a11cd5ca435acc9bb6523536%3Fs%3D84&amp;r=G" width="84"/>
                   <cite class="fn">
                    Jon Hanford
                   </cite>
                  </div>
                  <div class="commenttext">
                   <p>
                    @6@22@48, That unusual crater youre commenting on is Tsiolkovskiy:
                    <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Tsiolkovskiy_%28crater%29" rel="nofollow">
                     http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Tsiolkovskiy_%28crater%29
                    </a>
                   </p>
                   <p>
                    Interesting Tsiolkovskiy trivia:  Apollo 17 Astronaut Harrison Jack Schmitt  and other scientists (Schmitt was the only trained scientist, a geologist, to walk on the moon) strongly advocated Tsiolkovskiy as the Apollo 17s, or a later flights (which were all canceled), landing site, using small communications satellites deployed from the Command/Service Module for communication from the far side of the moon. NASA vetoed the idea as too risky, and Apollo 17 instead landed in the Taurus-Littrow valley on December 11, 1972.  wiki
                   </p>
                   <div class="comment-meta commentmetadata">
                    <span class="commentdate">
                     <a href="http://blogs.discovermagazine.com/badastronomy/2010/09/09/side-view-of-the-moon/#comment-253125">
                      September 11, 2010 at 12:45 pm
                     </a>
                    </span>
                    <span class="reply">
                    </span>
                   </div>
                  </div>
                 </div>
                </div>
               </li>
               <!-- #comment-## -->
               <li class="comment even thread-even depth-1" id="li-comment-253126">
                <div class="commentwrap clearfix">
                 <div class="commentBox clearfix" id="comment-253126">
                  <div class="comment-author vcard">
                   <img alt="" class="avatar avatar-84 photo" height="84" src="http://1.gravatar.com/avatar/b314a26a902a9db3da5f9c840470f445?s=84&amp;d=http%3A%2F%2F1.gravatar.com%2Favatar%2Fad516503a11cd5ca435acc9bb6523536%3Fs%3D84&amp;r=G" width="84"/>
                   <cite class="fn">
                    NelC
                   </cite>
                  </div>
                  <div class="commenttext">
                   <p>
                    Curt @49: from the point of view of Earth, thats apparently so, but the situation is complicated by the fact that the Earth orbits around the Sun while the Moon orbits around the Earth. From the point of view of an observer on the Sun or from the (almost) unmoving stars, if the Moon spun at exactly the same rate as its orbit around Earth, after six months it would have turned completely around and would be showing the opposite face to Earth. So (if Ive got it the right way around) it must spin a little faster than it orbits to maintain the same face to the Earth.
                   </p>
                   <div class="comment-meta commentmetadata">
                    <span class="commentdate">
                     <a href="http://blogs.discovermagazine.com/badastronomy/2010/09/09/side-view-of-the-moon/#comment-253126">
                      September 11, 2010 at 11:03 pm
                     </a>
                    </span>
                    <span class="reply">
                    </span>
                   </div>
                  </div>
                 </div>
                </div>
               </li>

           /* &lt;![CDATA[ */
		(function() {
			var dropdown = document.getElementById('discoverblog_categories');
			function onCatChange() {
				if ( dropdown.options[dropdown.selectedIndex].value &gt; 0 ) {
					location.href = "http://blogs.discovermagazine.com/badastronomy/?cat="+dropdown.options[dropdown.selectedIndex].value;
				}
			}
			dropdown.onchange = onCatChange;
		})();

		(function() {
			var dropdown = document.getElementById('discoverblog_tags');
			function onCatChange() {
				if (dropdown.options[dropdown.selectedIndex].value) {
					location.href = dropdown.options[dropdown.selectedIndex].value;
				}
			}
			dropdown.onchange = onCatChange;
		})();

		(function() {
			var dropdown = document.getElementById('discoverblog_archives');
			function onCatChange() {
				if (dropdown.options[dropdown.selectedIndex].value) {
					location.href = dropdown.options[dropdown.selectedIndex].value;
				}
			}
			dropdown.onchange = onCatChange;
		})();
	/* ]]&gt; */
